Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
6 min
The Musharrakah Dispute: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inheritance case with exclusions and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the different positions of the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding full brothers in the Musharrakah case and evaluate the evidence each side presents.

Core Idea
The inclusion of full brothers in the uterine brothers' 1/3 is disputed due to conflicting athar and principles of priority.

The Musharrakah case is not only an inheritance calculation but also a classical ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. In this lesson, we explore each school's proof and the practical consequence of their difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Full brothers are excluded (hajb hirman); they receive nothing because they are asabah and no residue remains after prescribed shares.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
2
State the Shafi'i Position
Full brothers share equally with uterine brothers in the 1/3 share, based on their closer relationship to the deceased.
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that full brothers are more entitled than uterine brothers to the maternal share.
3
Compare the Evidence
Hanafi relies on athar from Ibrahim reporting that Ali and Abu Bakr excluded full brothers. Shafi'i relies on athar from Shuraih, Masruq, and Uthman who included them.
Both sets of athar are from the Companions, creating a legitimate ikhtilaf.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
In Hanafi, full brothers receive 0; in Shafi'i, they receive a portion of the 1/3 (e.g., if one uterine and one full brother, each gets 1/6 of the estate).
This difference materially affects the distribution to the full brothers.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Samarqandi deals with the famous inheritance case known as 'al-Musharrakah' (also 'al-Musharikah'). The scenario: a deceased woman leaves a husband, a mother, several uterine brothers (same mother), and several full brothers (same father and mother). The passage concludes by noting that this is a well-known disputed issue, and the Hanafi school adheres to the exclusion of full brothers, which is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

حقیقی (ḥaqīqī): Full brother (same father and mother).
عصبہ (ʿaṣabah): Residuary heir(s) who take the remainder after prescribed shares are distributed.

Hajb Nuqsan at a Glance
Table of Hajb Nuqsan for various heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the share of a husband, wife, mother, granddaughter, or half-sister when other heirs are present, using the rules of Hajb Nuqsan.

Core Idea
An heir's share decreases when a closer or additional heir exists, but never to zero.

The passage provides a quick-reference table for how shares of common heirs change in the presence of others. Using this table, you can quickly determine the reduced share for each heir based on the family composition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Heir
Determine which heir you are calculating for (husband, wife, mother, granddaughter, or half-sister).
Heirs include husband, wife, mother, granddaughter, and half-sister in this table.
2
Check for Blocking Heirs
For each heir, there are specific relatives whose presence reduces the share. For example, the husband's share reduces if children exist.
Blocking heirs are listed in the table (children, grandchildren, siblings, etc.).
3
Apply the Reduced Share
Using the table, find the appropriate share percentage. For example, husband without children gets half (50%), with children gets quarter (25%).
Shares are fractions: 1/2, 1/4, 1/6, 1/8 etc.
4
State the Reason (Hajb Nuqsan)
Explain that this is Hajb Nuqsan: the share is reduced but not eliminated due to the presence of another heir.
This contrasts with Hajb Hirman where an heir is entirely excluded.

Chapter of Hajb. In this case, the uterine brothers who were given one third (33.33) — from that, half was given to the full brother. Because of this, both (full brothers) got 16.66 and the uterine brothers got 16.66. Al-Mushtaraka: This case is called the mixed case. Because the full brothers are sometimes excluded and sometimes included. In one expression it is al-musharaka, meaning that some scholars included the full brother in the share of the uterine brother, and some did not. (See Hajb Nuqsan at a glance and Hajb Hirman at a glance on the following pages.) Marginal note: They used to include [the full brother], and Hadhrat Uthman included the full brother with the uterine brother. Hajb Nuqsan at a glance: some numbers. Serial number. Heir. In which condition how much will he get. (1) Husband: if no children, half; if children, quarter. (2) Wife: if no children, quarter; if children, one-eighth. (3) Mother: if no children, grandson, or two brothers, one-third. Cases: (4) Mother: if children, one-sixth. (5) Mother: if grandson/granddaughter, one-sixth. (6) Mother: if brothers/sisters, one-sixth. (7) Granddaughter: if no daughter, half; if one daughter, one-sixth; if two daughters, nothing; if a grandson is present, she becomes an 'asaba. (8) Half-sister: if no full sister, half; if one full sister, one-sixth to complete two-thirds; if two full sisters, nothing; if a brother is present, she becomes an 'asaba.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Samari discusses the rules of Hajb (reduction/exclusion) in inheritance law, focusing on both a specific problematic case and a summary table. The passage begins with the well-known Hanafi case of 'al-Mushtaraka' (the mixed case). In this case, uterine brothers (akhafi) are entitled to one-third of the estate. However, if there are full brothers (haqiqi), a dispute arises: some Companions, like Hadhrat Uthman, included the full brothers with the uterine brothers in that one-third, dividing it equally among all. Others excluded the full brothers entirely. The text explains that the term 'mushtaraka' refers to the fact that full brothers are sometimes included and sometimes excluded. The commentator notes that this is a point of ikhtilaf among the early jurists. The passage then presents a table summarizing 'Hajb Nuqsan' (reduction of shares) for several heirs. The table shows how the shares of the husband, wife, mother, granddaughter, and half-sister decrease when certain other heirs exist. For example, the husband's share drops from half to quarter when children are present; the wife's share drops from quarter to one-eighth; the mother's share drops from one-third to one-sixth in the presence of children, grandchildren, or multiple siblings. The granddaughter gets half if there is no daughter, one-sixth if there is one daughter to complete two-thirds, and nothing if there are two daughters (unless there is a son to make her 'asaba). The half-sister similarly gets half if no full sister, one-sixth if one full sister, nothing if two full sisters, and becomes 'asaba with a brother. The table entries are somewhat garbled but the rulings are standard Hanafi positions. The passage thus serves as a practical reference for understanding how inheritance shares are modified by the presence of other heirs, and it highlights the contested ruling of al-Mushtaraka.

Key Terms
حجب نقصان (hajb nuqsan): Reduction of an heir's share due to the presence of another heir, without total exclusion.
عصبة (asabah): A residuary heir who takes the remainder after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the table, what is the share of the mother when the deceased has a son?
A) One-third (33.33%)
B) One-sixth (16.67%)
C) One-half (50%)
D) One-eighth (12.5%)

Key Takeaway
Hajb Nuqsan reduces but does not eliminate; each heir's share depends on the specific blocking heirs present.

The al-Mushtaraka Case: Full Brothers and Uterine Brothers
Explains the problem of al-Mushtaraka

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute of al-Mushtaraka and state the Hanafi mufta bihi position on whether full brothers share with uterine brothers.

Core Idea
In al-Mushtaraka, full brothers are sometimes included with uterine brothers in the one-third share, but the Hanafi school excludes them.

The passage introduces the case of al-Mushtaraka, where there is a dispute among the Companions and later jurists about whether full brothers (haqiqi) inherit with uterine brothers (akhafi) in the absence of children and father. This case highlights the tension between strict qiyas and the practice of some early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Heirs
The heirs are uterine brothers (akhafi) and full brothers (haqiqi). No children or father present.
Uterine brothers share the same mother but not father; full brothers share both parents.
2
Recall the Quranic Share
According to Quran 4:12, uterine siblings inherit one-third (if more than one) when there is no child or father.
The verse specifically mentions 'kalala' (a person without descendants or ascendants) and gives siblings shares.
3
Identify the Dispute
The dispute is whether full brothers should also share in that one-third or be excluded entirely. Some Companions, like Caliph Uthman, included them; others excluded.
The term 'mushtaraka' (mixed) reflects the fact that sometimes they are included, sometimes excluded.
4
State the Hanafi Position
The relied-upon Hanafi position (from Abu Hanifa) is that full brothers are excluded from the one-third. The 1/3 goes entirely to uterine brothers.
The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reportedly agreed with inclusion? But the text suggests the madhhab's fatwa is on exclusion.
5
Note the Practical Difference
If full brothers are included, the 1/3 is divided equally among all brothers (both types). If excluded, only uterine brothers take the 1/3.
Thus the shares differ: uterine brothers get 1/3 if alone; if included with full brothers, each gets 1/6 (assuming two brothers total).

المشترکة (al-mushtaraka): A specific inheritance problem where full brothers may be included with uterine brothers in the one-third share, due to a dispute among the Companions.
حجب حرمان (hajb hirman): Total exclusion of an heir from inheritance.

Key Takeaway
The al-Mushtaraka case illustrates how a textually grounded ruling (exclusion of full brothers) can be challenged by Companion practice, but the Hanafi school adheres to the stronger evidence.

Faraid — Inheritance
beginner

Hajb: Understanding Reduction and Total Exclusion in Inheritance
Defines and lists the two types of hijab with tables of heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hajb al-nuqsan and hajb al-hirman and identify which heirs are reduced or excluded according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Hajb is not just total exclusion—it also includes reduction of shares when other heirs are present.

This passage from Sharh al-Samiri presents two key aspects of hijab in Hanafi inheritance law. First, it details specific cases where an heir's share is reduced due to the presence of others (hajb al-nuqsan). Second, it categorizes those who are permanently excluded (hajb al-hirman) and those who can exclude others but are never excluded themselves. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly distributing an estate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hijab
Recognize that hajb al-nuqsan reduces an heir's share, while hajb al-hirman entirely excludes an heir.
These are distinct mechanisms with different effects.
2
Study the hajb al-nuqsan table
For each listed heir, note the condition that triggers a reduction and the resulting share. For example, husband reduces from 1/2 to 1/4 if deceased has children.
Memorize the specific conditions for key heirs: husband, wife, mother, granddaughter, and consanguine sister.
3
Understand the two subcategories of hajb al-hirman
First, six heirs (son, father, husband, daughter, mother, wife) can exclude others but are never excluded themselves. Second, five categories (non-Muslim, homicide, slave, apostate, difference of abodes) are always excluded.
These are foundational to all inheritance calculations.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a hypothetical family composition, determine which heirs are reduced or excluded using the tables.
Practice ensures retention.

(۲) قاتل: مقتول کا وارث نہیں ہوتا
(۳) غلام یا باندی: کسی کے وارث نہیں ہوتے
(۴) مرتد: کسی کا وارث نہیں ہوتا
(۵) اختلاف دارین: دار الاسلام والا دار الحرب والے کا وارث نہیں ہوگا
Chapter of Hijab

Hajb al-Nuqsan at a glance
Number | Heir | In what condition how much will receive
(1) Husband: if no child, half; (2) Husband: if child, quarter; (3) Wife: if no child, quarter; (4) Wife: if child, one-eighth; (5) Mother: if no child, grandson, and two brothers, one-third; (6) Mother: if child, one-sixth; (7) Mother: if grandson/daughter, one-sixth; (8) Mother: if brother/sister, one-sixth; (9) Granddaughter: if no son's daughter, half; (10) Granddaughter: if one son's daughter, one-sixth; (11) Granddaughter: if two daughters, nothing; (12) Granddaughter: if with grandson, becomes residuary; (13) One consanguine sister: if no full sister, half; (14) Consanguine sister: if one full sister, one-sixth; (15) Consanguine sister: if two full sisters, nothing; (19) Consanguine sister: if with consanguine brother, becomes residuary.

Hajb al-Hirman has two types.

At a glance
(1) One type: those who exclude others but themselves are not excluded from inheritance; rather they receive as a sharer or residuary. These are six persons: (1) Son, (2) Father, (3) Husband, (4) Daughter, (5) Mother, (6) Wife. They exclude others but themselves inherit as residuary or sharer.
(2) Second type: those who are forever excluded from inheritance. There are five types: (1) Non-Muslim, (2) Homicide, (3) Slave, (4) Apostate, (5) Difference of abodes. They are not heirs of the deceased's wealth.

Number | Heir | How received
(1) Son: always takes as residuary; (2) Father: sometimes as sharer, sometimes as residuary; (3) Husband: always as sharer, not as residuary; (4) Daughter: as sharer, and with a son as residuary; (5) Mother: always as sharer; (6) Wife: always as sharer.

(Always excluded)
(1) Non-Muslim: does not inherit from a Muslim; (2) Homicide: does not inherit from the victim; (3) Slave or slave-girl: do not inherit from anyone; (4) Apostate: does not inherit from anyone; (5) Difference of abodes: one in Dar al-Islam and the other in Dar al-Harb do not inherit from each other.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Samiri presents two key aspects of hijab (blocking) in Hanafi inheritance law: hajb al-nuqsan (reduction of share) and hajb al-hirman (total exclusion). The first table outlines specific cases where an heir's share is reduced due to the presence of other heirs. For example, a husband normally gets half, but if the deceased has children, his share reduces to a quarter. Similarly, a mother's share is one-third if no children or siblings, but reduces to one-sixth if children or multiple siblings exist. The second part categorizes hijab into two types: those who can exclude others but are never excluded themselves (like son, father, husband, daughter, mother, wife), and those who are permanently excluded (non-Muslims, murderers, slaves, apostates, and those from different dar al-harb). The tables serve as memorization anchors for students of faraid. The Hanafi position is that these rules are derived from the Qur'an and Sunnah, and this text follows the standard Hanafi doctrine without presenting intra-school disputes.

Key Terms
حجب (hajb): The legal mechanism by which the presence of one heir excludes or reduces the share of another heir.
حجب حرمان (hajb al-hirmān): Total exclusion from inheritance, e.g., a non-Muslim cannot inherit from a Muslim.
حجب نقصان (hajb al-nuqṣān): Reduction of an heir's share due to presence of other heirs, e.g., husband's share from half to quarter.
عصبہ (ʿaṣabah): Residuary heir who takes whatever remains after fixed shares are distributed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among the following is always excluded from inheritance?
A) The son of the deceased
B) The wife of the deceased
C) A non-Muslim relative
D) The mother of the deceased

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, hijab operates in two ways: reduction of shares (nuqsan) and total exclusion (hirman); six key heirs always receive a share, while five categories are permanently barred.

beginner

Types of Hajb (Exclusion) in Hanafi Inheritance
Classification of heirs who block others vs those permanently excluded

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of hajb hirman and identify which heirs permanently exclude others and which are permanently excluded.

Core Idea
In Hanafi inheritance, some heirs block others but still inherit themselves; others are permanently barred.

The Hanafi school classifies exclusion from inheritance (hajb hirman) into two fundamental categories. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly determining who inherits and who does not. This lesson outlines the six heirs who can block others but still receive a share, and the five individuals permanently excluded due to legal barriers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hajb hirman
Read the passage and note the two broad categories: heirs who block others but themselves inherit, and heirs permanently excluded.
Hajb hirman means total exclusion.
2
List the six heirs in the first category
Memorize the six: son, father, husband, daughter, mother, wife. Understand that each of these excludes certain others from inheritance.
They are never fully excluded themselves.
3
Specify how each of the six inherits
For each of the six, determine whether they take a fixed share (fard), as a residuary (asabah), or both. For example, husband always takes a fixed share; son always takes as asabah; father may take both depending on presence of son.
Refer to the table in the passage.
4
List the five permanently excluded heirs
Memorize the five permanent barriers: disbelief, killing, slavery, apostasy, and difference of abode. These individuals can never inherit from the deceased.
Even if they are close relatives.
5
Understand the reason for permanent exclusion
For each of the five permanent exclusions, explain why the barrier prevents inheritance. For example, a disbeliever cannot inherit from a Muslim because of difference in religion.
These are man'i' (preventers) in Hanafi faraid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحجب حجب حرمان کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ حجب حرمان ایک نظر میں (۱) ایک تو یہ کہ دوسروں کو محروم کرتے ہیں لیکن خود وراثت سے محروم نہیں ہوتے ۔ بلکہ حصے کے طور پر یا عصبہ کے طور پر مل ہی جاتی ہے۔ یہ چھ قسم کے لوگ ہیں (۱) بیٹا (۲) باپ (۳) شوہر (۴) بیٹی (۵) ماں (۶) بیوی۔ یہ دوسروں کو محروم کرتے ہیں لیکن خود عصبہ یا حصے کے طور پر وراثت لے لیتے ہیں۔ (۲) دوسری قسم وہ لوگ جو ہمیشہ کے لئے وراثت سے محروم ہو جاتے ہیں۔ وہ پانچ قسم کے لوگ ہیں (۱) کافر (۲) قاتل (۳) غلام (۴) مرند (۵) اختلاف دارین۔ یہ میت کے مال کے ورث نہیں ہوتے ۔ ( کسی حال میں محروم نہیں ہوتے ) نمبر شمار حصے دار کس طرح ملتا ہے (1) بیٹا ہمیشہ عصبہ کے طور پر لیتا ہے (۲) باپ حصے کے طور پر، اور کبھی عصبہ کے طور پر (۳) شوہر ہمیشہ حصے کے طور پر ، عصبہ کے طور پر نہیں (۴) بیٹی حصے کے طور پر، اور اس کے ساتھ بیٹا ہو تو عصبہ کے طور پر (۵) ماں ہمیشہ حصے کے طور پر (1) بیوی ہمیشہ حصے کے طور پر ( ہمیشہ محروم ہوتے ہیں) نمبر شمار حصے دار کس طرح ملتا ہے (1) کافر مسلمان کا وارث نہیں ہوتا (۲) قاتل مقتول کا وارث نہیں ہوتا (۳) غلام یا باندی کسی کے وارث نہیں ہوتے (۴) مرند کسی کا وارث نہیں ہوتا (۵) اختلاف دارین دار الاسلام والا دار الحرب والے کا وارث نہیں ہو گا
Chapter of Hajb. There are two types of exclusion (hajb hirman). First type: those who exclude others but are not themselves excluded from inheritance; rather they receive as a share or as an asabah. These are six persons: son, father, husband, daughter, mother, wife. They exclude others but themselves inherit as asabah or by share. Second type: those who are permanently excluded from inheritance. They are five: disbeliever, killer, slave, apostate, difference of abode (dar al-Islam vs dar al-harb). These do not inherit from the deceased. (Those who are never excluded:) Number: heir, how they take: (1) son always as asabah, (2) father by share and sometimes as asabah, (3) husband always by share, never as asabah, (4) daughter by share and if with a son then as asabah, (5) mother always by share, (6) wife always by share. (Those always excluded:) Number: heir, how they take: (1) disbeliever does not inherit from a Muslim, (2) killer does not inherit from the killed, (3) male or female slave do not inherit from anyone, (4) apostate does not inherit from anyone, (5) difference of abode: a person from dar al-Islam does not inherit from a person from dar al-harb and vice versa.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage introduces the two fundamental categories of exclusion (hajb) in Hanafi inheritance law. The first category are heirs who, by their presence, block others from receiving inheritance, but they themselves still receive a share—either as a fixed share (fard) or as a residuary (asabah). The passage lists six such heirs: the son, father, husband, daughter, mother, and wife. Each has a specific mode of inheritance: for example, the son always takes as an asabah (residuary), while the husband always takes a fixed share and never as asabah. The second category are individuals who are permanently excluded from inheritance due to a barrier (man'): kufr (disbelief), qatl (killing), riqq (slavery), riddah (apostasy), or ikhtilaf al-darayn (difference of Islamic and non-Islamic territories). These persons can never inherit from the deceased, even if they are otherwise entitled by blood relation. The passage organizes these rules in a clear tabular format for easy memorization.

Key Terms
حجب حرمان (hajb hirman): Exclusion from inheritance due to a legal barrier, either by another heir or by a permanent cause.
عصبہ (asabah): Residuary heirs who take the remaining inheritance after fixed shares are given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rules of hajb, which of the following is an example of a person who is permanently excluded from inheritance?
A) Son
B) Husband
C) Slave
D) Mother

Key Takeaway
The two categories of hajb hirman are: six heirs who block others but themselves inherit, and five individuals permanently excluded due to disbelief, killing, slavery, apostasy, or difference of abode.

Radd (Return of Surplus) in Inheritance: Hanafi Ruling and Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling, evidence, and dispute with Shafi'i on returning surplus inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on radd, identify the evidence from the athar, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
Surplus inheritance returns to all fixed-share heirs except spouses, as practiced by Ali.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of radd (return of surplus) in Islamic inheritance law. The Hanafi school holds that when there is no residuary heir (asabah), the remaining estate is redistributed among the fixed-share heirs proportionally, excluding the husband and wife. This ruling is based on an athar from Sayyiduna Ali. The Shafi'i school, however, argues that the surplus should go to the public treasury. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical distribution of estates.

Guided Path
1
Define radd and its conditions
Identify the legal definition: return of surplus to fixed-share heirs when no asabah exists, and note the exception of spouses.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
Locate the athar from Ibrahim that Ali practiced radd for all except spouses, and recognize its source (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba).
3
Contrast with Shafi'i proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: another athar that Zayd ibn Thabit placed surplus in bayt al-mal and that Companions did not give radd to spouses.
4
Analyze the dispute
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi returns surplus to heirs (more shares), Shafi'i gives to bayt al-mal (state).
Both sides rely on athar from different Companions.
5
Apply the Hanafi ruling to an example
Work through the given numeric example: deceased woman with husband and two daughters, calculate shares with radd.
Use the arithmetic from the passage: total 100, husband 25, daughters 66.66, radd 8.34 given to daughters.

Chapter of Radd. Necessary note: In this chapter, besides radd, many other issues are mentioned. Therefore the title 'Bāb al-Radd' is a partial name. The meaning of radd is to return. The share-takers take their shares, but some part remains, and there is no asabah nor dhawi al-arham, so the remaining portion is redistributed to the remaining lineage and family sharers according to their shares. This is called radd. Since the husband and wife are not blood relatives nor sharers, after taking their shares, they are not given anything again; they suffice with their shares. There is evidence for this from an athar: 'From Ibrahim that Ali used to give radd to every share-holder except the husband and the wife.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 260 fi al-radd wa ikhtilafihim fih, vol. 6, p. 255, no. 31159). From this athar it is known that besides the wife and husband, the remaining share-holders are given the property according to their shares. [3229] (1) Whatever property remains after the shares of the share-holders, when there is no asabah, it is returned to the share-holders according to their shares, except the wife and husband. If there is no [asabah?], then it is done accordingly. And the case: if there is no asabah, then however many share-holders there are, the remaining property is divided among them according to their shares, except the wife and husband. The case is formed like this: Deceased woman leaves: husband and two daughters. The husband due to the presence of children gets one-quarter, i.e., 25 from 100. The daughters get two-thirds, i.e., 66.66. 8.34 remains. That remaining is given to the two daughters as radd, and the husband is not given because the athar prohibits giving to him. Imam Shafi'i says: whatever remains should be given to the bayt al-mal (public treasury); do not return it to the share-holders. Their evidence is this athar: 'Ibrahim said: None of the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave radd to the wife or husband. He said: And Zayd used to give every fixed-share heir his share, and whatever remained he would place in the bayt al-mal.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 26 fi al-radd wa ikhtilafihim fih, vol. 6, p. 256, footnote: (a) Ali used to give the remaining property again to every share-holder except the husband and wife. (b) Ibrahim al-Nakha'i said that the Companions of the Prophet (continued on next page).
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri explains the Hanafi ruling on radd (return of surplus) in inheritance. The author first clarifies that the chapter title is partial because it includes more than just radd. He defines radd as redistributing the leftover estate among the fixed-share heirs (ashab al-furud) when there is no asabah (residuary) and no dhawi al-arham (distant kindred). The key exception is that the spouse (husband or wife) does not receive any radd because they are not blood relatives; they are connected by marital tie, not nasab. The Hanafi school relies on the athar (tradition) of Sayyiduna Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) who practiced radd for all except spouses. This is cited from the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba. Then the author states the ruling in matn-style: the surplus is returned to the share-holders except the spouses. He gives a concrete example: a woman dies leaving a husband and two daughters. The husband gets 1/4 (25 out of 100), the daughters get 2/3 (66.66), and the remaining 8.34 is given to the daughters as radd. The husband is excluded from radd. The author then presents the counter-opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the surplus should go to the bayt al-mal (public treasury) instead of being returned to the heirs, based on another athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that the Companions did not give radd to spouses and that Zayd ibn Thabit would place the surplus in the treasury. The passage ends with a footnote that continues on the next page. This shows the Hanafi preference for the practice of Ali over Zayd, and the response to Shafi'i is implied: the practice of Ali is stronger because it is more widely transmitted or because the preservation of the inherited property within the family is preferred.

Key Terms
رد (radd): Return of surplus inheritance to fixed-share heirs when there is no asabah.
اثر (athar): Narration from a Companion or Tabi'i, used as legal proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which heirs are excluded from receiving radd?
A) All male heirs
B) Husband and wife
C) Children
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that surplus inheritance is returned to all fixed-share heirs except the spouse, based on the practice of Ali.
